     <p>
      The
      <b>
       Raspberry Pi
      </b>
      is a series of
      <a href="/wiki/Credit_card" title="Credit card">
       credit card
      </a>
      -sized
      <a href="/wiki/Single-board_computer" title="Single-board computer">
       single-board computers
      </a>
      developed in the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
       UK
      </a>
      by the
      <a href="/wiki/Raspberry_Pi_Foundation" title="Raspberry Pi Foundation">
       Raspberry Pi Foundation
      </a>
      with the intention of promoting the teaching of basic
      <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">
       computer science
      </a>
      in schools.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The original Raspberry Pi and Raspberry Pi 2 are manufactured in several board configurations through licensed manufacturing agreements with
      <a href="/wiki/Newark_element14" title="Newark element14">
       Newark element14
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Premier_Farnell" title="Premier Farnell">
       Premier Farnell
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/RS_Components" title="RS Components">
       RS Components
      </a>
      and Egoman. These companies sell the Raspberry Pi online.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Egoman produces a version for distribution solely in China and Taiwan, which can be distinguished from other Pis by their red colouring and lack of
      <a href="/wiki/FCC_Declaration_of_Conformity" title="FCC Declaration of Conformity">
       FCC
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/CE_marking" title="CE marking">
       CE marks
      </a>
      . The hardware is the same across all manufacturers.
     </p>
     <p>
      The original Raspberry Pi is based on the
      <a href="/wiki/Broadcom" title="Broadcom">
       Broadcom
      </a>
      BCM2835
      <a href="/wiki/System_on_a_chip" title="System on a chip">
       system on a chip
      </a>
      (SoC),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadcom-BCM2835-Website_1-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Broadcom-BCM2835-Website-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which includes an
      <a href="/wiki/ARM11" title="ARM11">
       ARM1176JZF-S
      </a>
      700
      <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">
       MHz
      </a>
      processor,
      <a href="/wiki/VideoCore" title="VideoCore">
       VideoCore
      </a>
      IV GPU,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grandmax_brose_2012_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-grandmax_brose_2012-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and was originally shipped with 256megabytes of
      <a href="/wiki/Random-access_memory" title="Random-access memory">
       RAM
      </a>
      , later upgraded (models B and B+) to 512
      <a href="/wiki/Mebibyte" title="Mebibyte">
       MB
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MB_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MB-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-512MB_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-512MB-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The system has
      <a href="/wiki/Secure_Digital" title="Secure Digital">
       Secure Digital
      </a>
      (SD) (models A and B) or MicroSD (models A+ and B+) sockets for boot media and persistent storage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VerifiedPeripheralList_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-VerifiedPeripheralList-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2014, the Raspberry Pi Foundation launched the
      <i>
       Compute Module
      </i>
      , which packages a BCM2835 with 512MB RAM and an
      <a href="/wiki/MultiMediaCard#eMMC" title="MultiMediaCard">
       eMMC
      </a>
      flash chip into a module for use as a part of embedded systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Foundation provides Debian and Arch Linux ARM
      <a href="/wiki/Linux_distribution" title="Linux distribution">
       distributions
      </a>
      for download.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raspberrypi_downloads_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-raspberrypi_downloads-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tools are available for
      <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">
       Python
      </a>
      as the main programming language, with support for
      <a href="/wiki/BBC_BASIC" title="BBC BASIC">
       BBC BASIC
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edge_david_braben_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-edge_david_braben-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (via the
      <a href="/wiki/RISC_OS" title="RISC OS">
       RISC OS
      </a>
      image or the Brandy Basic clone for Linux),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">
       C
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">
       C++
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">
       Java
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-java_on_raspberry_pi_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-java_on_raspberry_pi-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">
       Perl
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ruby_(programming_language)" title="Ruby (programming language)">
       Ruby
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raspberry_Pi_GPIO_Programming_with_pi_piper_Ruby_gem_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Raspberry_Pi_GPIO_Programming_with_pi_piper_Ruby_gem-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As of 18 February 2015
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , over five million Raspberry Pis have been sold.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While already the fastest selling British
      <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">
       personal computer
      </a>
      , it has also shipped the second largest number of units behind the
      <a href="/wiki/Amstrad_PCW" title="Amstrad PCW">
       Amstrad PCW
      </a>
      , the "Personal Computer
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Word-processor" title="Word-processor">
       Word-processor
      </a>
      ", which sold eight million.
     </p>
     <p>
      In early February 2015, the next-generation Raspberry Pi, Raspberry Pi 2, was released.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The new computer board is initially available only in one configuration (model B) and features a Broadcom BCM2836 SoC, with a
      <a href="/wiki/Multi-core_processor" title="Multi-core processor">
       quad-core
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/ARM_Cortex-A7" title="ARM Cortex-A7">
       ARM Cortex-A7
      </a>
      CPU and a VideoCore IV dual-core GPU; 1GB of RAM with remaining specifications being similar to those of the previous generation model B+. Crucially, the Raspberry Pi 2 retains the same US$35 price point of the model B,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_RaspPi_2_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBC_RaspPi_2-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      with the US$20 model A+ remaining on sale.
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hardware">
       Hardware
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Hardware">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raspberrypi_block_function_v01.svg">
       <img alt="Raspberrypi block function v01.svg" data-file-height="307" data-file-width="720" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Raspberrypi_block_function_v01.svg/220px-Raspberrypi_block_function_v01.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Raspberrypi_block_function_v01.svg/330px-Raspberrypi_block_function_v01.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Raspberrypi_block_function_v01.svg/440px-Raspberrypi_block_function_v01.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
      </a>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:left;">
     </div>
     <p>
      In the above block diagram for model
      <i>
       A, B, A+, B+
      </i>
      ; model
      <i>
       A
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       A+
      </i>
      have the lowest two blocks and the rightmost block missing (note that these three blocks are in a chip that actually contains a three-port USB hub, with a USB Ethernet adapter connected to one of its ports). In model
      <i>
       A
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       A+
      </i>
      the USB port is connected directly to the SoC. On model
      <i>
       B+
      </i>
      the chip contains a five-point hub, with four USB ports fed out, instead of the two on model
      <i>
       B
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Processor">
       Processor
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Processor">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/System_on_a_chip" title="System on a chip">
       SoC
      </a>
      used in the first generation Raspberry Pi is somewhat equivalent to the chip used in older
      <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">
       smartphones
      </a>
      (such as
      <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">
       iPhone
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/IPhone_3G" title="IPhone 3G">
       3G
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/IPhone_3GS" title="IPhone 3GS">
       3GS
      </a>
      ). The Raspberry Pi is based on the
      <a href="/wiki/Broadcom" title="Broadcom">
       Broadcom
      </a>
      BCM2835
      <a href="/wiki/System_on_a_chip" title="System on a chip">
       system on a chip
      </a>
      (SoC),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadcom-BCM2835-Website_1-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Broadcom-BCM2835-Website-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which includes an 700
      <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">
       MHz
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/ARM11" title="ARM11">
       ARM1176JZF-S
      </a>
      processor,
      <a href="/wiki/VideoCore" title="VideoCore">
       VideoCore
      </a>
      IV GPU,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grandmax_brose_2012_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-grandmax_brose_2012-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and RAM. It has a Level 1
      <a href="/wiki/CPU_cache" title="CPU cache">
       cache
      </a>
      of 16
      <a href="/wiki/Kibibyte" title="Kibibyte">
       KB
      </a>
      and a Level 2
      <a href="/wiki/CPU_cache" title="CPU cache">
       cache
      </a>
      of 128
      <a href="/wiki/Kibibyte" title="Kibibyte">
       KB
      </a>
      . The Level 2 cache is used primarily by the GPU. The SoC is
      <a href="/wiki/Package_on_package" title="Package on package">
       stacked
      </a>
      underneath the RAM chip, so only its edge is visible.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Performance_of_first_generation_models">
       Performance of first generation models
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Performance of first generation models">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      While operating at 700MHz by default, the first generation Raspberry Pi provided a real world performance roughly equivalent to 0.041
      <a href="/wiki/FLOPS" title="FLOPS">
       GFLOPS
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eLinux-perf_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-eLinux-perf-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hackaday-raspi_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hackaday-raspi-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the
      <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">
       CPU
      </a>
      level the performance is similar to a 300MHz
      <a href="/wiki/Pentium_II" title="Pentium II">
       Pentium II
      </a>
      of 1997-1999. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPU" title="GPU">
       GPU
      </a>
      provides 1
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gpixel" title="Gpixel">
       Gpixel
      </a>
      /s or 1.5
      <a href="/wiki/Texel_(graphics)" title="Texel (graphics)">
       Gtexel
      </a>
      /s of graphics processing or 24GFLOPS of general purpose computing performance. The graphics capabilities of the Raspberry Pi are roughly equivalent to the level of performance of the
      <a href="/wiki/Xbox_(console)" title="Xbox (console)">
       Xbox
      </a>
      of 2001.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/LINPACK" title="LINPACK">
       LINPACK
      </a>
      single node compute benchmark results in a mean
      <a href="/wiki/Single-precision_floating-point_format" title="Single-precision floating-point format">
       single precision performance
      </a>
      of 0.065GFLOPS and a mean
      <a href="/wiki/Double-precision_floating-point_format" title="Double-precision floating-point format">
       double precision performance
      </a>
      of 0.041GFLOPS for one Raspberry Pi Model-B board.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raspicluster-perf_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-raspicluster-perf-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A cluster of 64 Raspberry Pi Model-B computers, labeled "Iridis-pi", achieved a LINPACK
      <a href="/wiki/LINPACK_benchmarks#HPL" title="LINPACK benchmarks">
       HPL
      </a>
      suite result of 1.14GFLOPS (n=10240) at 216
      <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">
       watts
      </a>
      for c. US$4,000.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raspicluster-perf_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-raspicluster-perf-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Raspberry Pi 2 is based on Broadcom BCM2836 SoC, which includes a quad core Cortex-A7 CPU running at 900MHz and 1GB RAM. It is described as 4-6 times more powerful than its predecessor. The GPU is identical.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Overclocking">
       Overclocking
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Overclocking">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The first generation Raspberry Pi chip operated at 700MHz by default and did not become hot enough to need a
      <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">
       heat sink
      </a>
      or special cooling, unless the chip was overclocked. The second generation runs at 900MHz by default, and also does not become hot enough to need a heatsink or special cooling. Again
      <a href="/wiki/Overclocking" title="Overclocking">
       overclocking
      </a>
      may heat up the SoC more than usual.
     </p>
     <p>
      Most Raspberry Pi chips could be overclocked to 800MHz and some even higher to 1000MHz. There are reports the second generation can be similarly overclocked, in extreme cases, even to 1500MHz (discarding all safety features and over voltage limitations). In the
      <a href="#Software">
       Raspbian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linux_distro" title="Linux distro">
       Linux distro
      </a>
      the overclocking options on
      <a href="/wiki/Booting" title="Booting">
       boot
      </a>
      can be done by a software command running "sudo raspi-config" without voiding the warranty.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In those cases the Pi automatically shuts the overclocking down in case the chip reaches 85C (185F), but it is possible to overrule automatic over voltage and overclocking settings (voiding the warranty). In that case, one can try putting an appropriately sized heatsink on it to keep the chip from heating up far above 85C.
     </p>
     <p>
      Newer versions of the firmware contain the option to choose between five overclock ("turbo") presets that when turned on try to get the most performance out of the SoC without impairing the lifetime of the Pi. This is done by monitoring the core temperature of the chip, and the CPU load, and dynamically adjusting clock speeds and the core voltage. When the demand is low on the CPU, or it is running too hot, the performance is throttled, but if the CPU has much to do, and the chip's temperature is acceptable, performance is temporarily increased, with clock speeds of up to 1GHz, depending on the individual board, and on which of the turbo settings is used. The five settings are:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       none; 700MHz ARM, 250MHz core, 400MHz SDRAM, 0 overvolt,
      </li>
      <li>
       modest; 800MHz ARM, 250MHz core, 400MHz SDRAM, 0 overvolt,
      </li>
      <li>
       medium; 900MHz ARM, 250MHz core, 450MHz SDRAM, 2 overvolt,
      </li>
      <li>
       high; 950MHz ARM, 250MHz core, 450MHz SDRAM, 6 overvolt,
      </li>
      <li>
       turbo; 1000MHz ARM, 500MHz core, 600MHz SDRAM, 6 overvolt.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turbo_mode_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Turbo_mode-23">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         23
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turbosgithub_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-turbosgithub-24">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         24
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      In the highest (
      <i>
       turbo
      </i>
      ) preset the SDRAM clock was originally 500MHz, but this was later changed to 600MHz because 500MHz sometimes causes SD card corruption. Simultaneously in
      <i>
       high
      </i>
      mode the core clock speed was lowered from 450 to 250MHz, and in
      <i>
       medium
      </i>
      mode from 333 to 250MHz.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="RAM">
       RAM
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: RAM">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      On the older beta model B boards, 128MB was allocated by default to the GPU, leaving 128MB for the CPU.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the first 256MB release model B (and model A), three different splits were possible. The default split was 192MB (RAM for CPU), which should be sufficient for standalone 1080p video decoding, or for simple 3D, but probably not for both together. 224MB was for Linux only, with just a 1080p
      <a href="/wiki/Framebuffer" title="Framebuffer">
       framebuffer
      </a>
      , and was likely to fail for any video or 3D. 128MB was for heavy 3D, possibly also with video decoding (e.g. XBMC).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Comparatively the Nokia 701 uses 128MB for the Broadcom VideoCore IV.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For the new model B with 512MB RAM initially there were new standard memory split files released( arm256_start.elf, arm384_start.elf, arm496_start.elf) for 256MB, 384MB and 496MB CPU RAM (and 256MB, 128MB and 16MB video RAM). But a week or so later the RPF released a new version of start.elf that could read a new entry in config.txt (gpu_mem=xx) and could dynamically assign an amount of RAM (from 16 to 256MB in 8MB steps) to the GPU, so the older method of memory splits became obsolete, and a single start.elf worked the same for 256 and 512MB Raspberry Pis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The second generation has 1GB of RAM.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Networking">
       Networking
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Networking">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Though the model A and A+ do not have an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8P8C" title="8P8C">
       8P8C
      </a>
      ("RJ45") Ethernet port, they can be connected to a network using an external user-supplied USB Ethernet or
      <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">
       Wi-Fi
      </a>
      adapter. On the
      <span class="nowrap">
       model B and B+
      </span>
      the Ethernet port is provided by a built-in USB Ethernet adapter.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Peripherals">
       Peripherals
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Peripherals">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Generic USB
      <a href="/wiki/Computer_keyboard" title="Computer keyboard">
       keyboards
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mouse_(computing)" title="Mouse (computing)">
       mice
      </a>
      are compatible with the Raspberry Pi.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VerifiedPeripheralList_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-VerifiedPeripheralList-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Video">
       Video
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Video">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The video controller is capable of standard modern TV resolutions, such as HD and Full HD, and higher or lower monitor resolutions and older standard CRT TV resolutions; capable of the following: 640350
      <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_Graphics_Adapter" title="Enhanced Graphics Adapter">
       EGA
      </a>
      ; 640480
      <a href="/wiki/Video_Graphics_Array" title="Video Graphics Array">
       VGA
      </a>
      ; 800600
      <a href="/wiki/Super_video_graphics_array" title="Super video graphics array">
       SVGA
      </a>
      ; 1024768
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/XGA" title="XGA">
       XGA
      </a>
      ; 1280720
      <a href="/wiki/720p" title="720p">
       720p
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television#High-definition_display_resolutions" title="High-definition television">
       HDTV
      </a>
      ; 1280768
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graphic_display_resolutions#WXGA" title="Graphic display resolutions">
       WXGA
      </a>
      variant; 1280800
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graphic_display_resolutions#WXGA" title="Graphic display resolutions">
       WXGA
      </a>
      variant; 12801024
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SXGA" title="SXGA">
       SXGA
      </a>
      ; 1366768
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graphic_display_resolutions#WXGA" title="Graphic display resolutions">
       WXGA
      </a>
      variant; 14001050
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SXGA%2B" title="SXGA+">
       SXGA+
      </a>
      ; 16001200
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UXGA" title="UXGA">
       UXGA
      </a>
      ; 16801050
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WXGA%2B" title="WXGA+">
       WXGA+
      </a>
      ; 19201080
      <a href="/wiki/1080p" title="1080p">
       1080p
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television#High-definition_display_resolutions" title="High-definition television">
       HDTV
      </a>
      ; 19201200
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WUXGA" title="WUXGA">
       WUXGA
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-video_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-video-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It can generate
      <a href="/wiki/576i" title="576i">
       576i
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/480i" title="480i">
       480i
      </a>
      composite video signals for
      <a href="/wiki/PAL#PAL-B.2FG.2FD.2FK.2FI" title="PAL">
       PAL-BGHID
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/PAL-M" title="PAL-M">
       PAL-M
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PAL-N" title="PAL-N">
       PAL-N
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/NTSC" title="NTSC">
       NTSC
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/NTSC-J" title="NTSC-J">
       NTSC-J
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-composite_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-composite-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Real-time_clock">
       Real-time clock
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Real-time clock">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Raspberry Pi does not come with a
      <a href="/wiki/Real-time_clock" title="Real-time clock">
       real-time clock
      </a>
      , which means it cannot keep track of the time of day while it is not powered on.
     </p>
     <p>
      As alternatives, a program running on the Pi can get the time from a
      <a href="/wiki/Network_Time_Protocol" title="Network Time Protocol">
       network time server
      </a>
      or user input at boot time.
     </p>
     <p>
      A real-time clock (such as the DS1307) with battery backup can be added (often via the
      <a href="/wiki/I%C2%B2C" title="IC">
       IC
      </a>
      interface).
     </p>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="GPIO_connector">
       GPIO connector
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: GPIO connector">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      RPi A+, B+ and 2B GPIO J8 40-pin pinout.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Models A and B have only the first 26 pins.
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable">
      <tr>
       <th>
        GPIO#
       </th>
       <th>
        2nd func
       </th>
       <th>
        pin#
       </th>
       <th>
       </th>
       <th>
        pin#
       </th>
       <th>
        2nd func
       </th>
       <th>
        GPIO#
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        N/A
       </td>
       <td>
        +3V3
       </td>
       <td style="background:orange">
        1
       </td>

     <p>
      Models A and B provide GPIO access to the ACT status LED using GPIO 16. Models A+ and B+ provide GPIO access to the ACT status LED using GPIO 47, and the power status LED using GPIO 35.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Accessories">
       Accessories
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Accessories">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Camera  On 14 May 2013, the foundation and the distributors RS Components &amp; Premier Farnell/Element 14 launched the Raspberry Pi camera board with a firmware update to accommodate it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         59
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The camera board is shipped with a
       <a href="/wiki/Flexible_flat_cable" title="Flexible flat cable">
        flexible flat cable
       </a>
       that plugs into the
       <a href="/wiki/Camera_interface" title="Camera interface">
        CSI
       </a>
       connector located between the Ethernet and HDMI ports. In Raspbian, one enables the system to use the camera board by the installing or upgrading to the latest version of the
       <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">
        operating system
       </a>
       (OS) and then running Raspi-config and selecting the camera option. The cost of the camera module is
       <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">
        
       </a>
       20 in Europe (9 September 2013).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farnellcam_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-farnellcam-60">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         60
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       It can produce
       <a href="/wiki/1080p" title="1080p">
        1080p
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/720p" title="720p">
        720p
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/480p" title="480p">
        640x480p
       </a>
       video. The footprint dimensions are
       <span class="nowrap">
        25 mm x 20 mm x 9 mm.
       </span>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-farnellcam_60-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-farnellcam-60">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         60
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Gertboard  A Raspberry Pi Foundation sanctioned device, designed for educational purposes, that expands the Raspberry Pi's GPIO pins to allow interface with and control of LEDs, switches, analog signals, sensors and other devices. It also includes an optional
       <a href="/wiki/Arduino" title="Arduino">
        Arduino
       </a>
       compatible controller to interface with the Pi.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gertboard_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gertboard-61">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         61
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Infrared Camera  In October 2013, the foundation announced that they would begin producing a camera module without an infrared filter, called the Pi NoIR.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         62
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       HAT (Hardware Attached on Top) expansion boards  Together with the model B+, inspired by the
       <a href="/wiki/Arduino#Shields" title="Arduino">
        Arduino shield
       </a>
       boards, the interface for HAT boards was devised by the Raspberry Pi Foundation. Each HAT board carries a small EEPROM (typically a CAT24C32WI-GT3)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         63
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       containing the relevant details of the board,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         64
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       so that the Raspberry Pi's OS is informed of the HAT, and the technical details of it, relevant to the OS using the HAT.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         65
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mechanical details of a HAT board, that use the four mounting holes in their rectangular formation, are here:
       <a class="external autonumber" href="https://github.com/raspberrypi/hats/blob/master/hat-board-mechanical.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        [1]
       </a>
       . More info here:
       <a class="external autonumber" href="https://github.com/raspberrypi/hats" rel="nofollow">
        [2]
       </a>
       .
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Software">
       Software
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Software">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Operating_systems">
       Operating systems
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Operating systems">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Raspberry Pi primarily uses
      <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">
       Linux-kernel
      </a>
      -based operating systems.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/ARM11" title="ARM11">
       ARM11
      </a>
      chip at the heart of the Pi (first generation models) is based on version 6 of the
      <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture" title="ARM architecture">
       ARM
      </a>
      . The current releases of several popular versions of Linux, including
      <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_(operating_system)" title="Ubuntu (operating system)">
       Ubuntu
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      will not run on the ARM11. It is not possible to run Windows on the original Raspberry Pi,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPIHQ_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RPIHQ-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      though the new Raspberry Pi 2 will be able to run
      <a href="/wiki/Windows_10" title="Windows 10">
       Windows 10
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wincom20150202_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-wincom20150202-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Raspberry Pi 2 currently only supports
      <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_(operating_system)" title="Ubuntu (operating system)">
       Ubuntu Snappy Core
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raspbian" title="Raspbian">
       Raspbian
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/OpenELEC" title="OpenELEC">
       OpenELEC
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/RISC_OS" title="RISC OS">
       RISC OS
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-golem.de_Raspberry_Pi_2_2015_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-golem.de_Raspberry_Pi_2_2015-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The install manager for the Raspberry Pi is NOOBS. The operating systems included with NOOBS are:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Arch_Linux_ARM" title="Arch Linux ARM">
        Archlinux ARM
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/OpenELEC" title="OpenELEC">
        OpenELEC
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcworld.fr_bellavance_2012_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pcworld.fr_bellavance_2012-70">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         70
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Pidora (
       <a href="/wiki/Fedora_(operating_system)" title="Fedora (operating system)">
        Fedora
       </a>
       Remix)
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Puppy_Linux" title="Puppy Linux">
        Puppy Linux
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         71
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raspbmc" title="Raspbmc">
        Raspbmc
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         72
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/XBMC" title="XBMC">
        XBMC
       </a>
       open source
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_media_receiver" title="Digital media receiver">
        digital media center
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XBMC_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-XBMC-73">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         73
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/RISC_OS" title="RISC OS">
        RISC OS
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osnews_risc_os_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-osnews_risc_os-74">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         74
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
        is the operating system of the first ARM-based computer.
      </li>
      <li>
       Raspbian (recommended for Raspberry Pi 1)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         75
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
        Maintained independently of the Foundation;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homepageRasp_76-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-homepageRasp-76">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         76
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       based on the
       <a href="/wiki/Debian#Architecture_ports" title="Debian">
        ARM hard-float
       </a>
       (armhf)
       <a href="/wiki/Debian" title="Debian">
        Debian
       </a>
       7 'Wheezy' architecture port originally designed for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARMv7" title="ARMv7">
        ARMv7
       </a>
       and later processors (with
       <a href="/wiki/Jazelle" title="Jazelle">
        Jazelle
       </a>
       RCT/ThumbEE,
       <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture#Floating-point_.28VFP.29" title="ARM architecture">
        VFPv3
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture#Advanced_SIMD_.28NEON.29" title="ARM architecture">
        NEON
       </a>
       SIMD extensions), compiled for the more limited
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARMv6" title="ARMv6">
        ARMv6
       </a>
       instruction set of the Raspberry Pi. A minimum size of 4
       <a href="/wiki/Gigabyte" title="Gigabyte">
        GB
       </a>
       SD card is required. There is a Pi Store for exchanging programs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_classroom_coders_77-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbc_classroom_coders-77">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         77
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         78
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <ul>
        <li>
         The Raspbian Server Edition is a stripped version with fewer software packages bundled as compared to the usual
         <a href="/wiki/Desktop_computer" title="Desktop computer">
          desktop computer
         </a>
         oriented Raspbian.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RaspbianServerEdition_79-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-RaspbianServerEdition-79">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           79
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPi-downloads_80-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-RPi-downloads-80">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           80
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </li>
        <li>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Wayland_(display_server_protocol)" title="Wayland (display server protocol)">
          Wayland display server protocol
         </a>
         enable the efficient use of the GPU for
         <a href="/wiki/Hardware_acceleration" title="Hardware acceleration">
          hardware accelerated
         </a>
         GUI drawing functions.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wayland_81-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-wayland-81">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           81
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         on 16 April 2014 a GUI shell for Weston called
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maynard_(software)" title="Maynard (software)">
          Maynard
         </a>
         was released.
        </li>
        <li>
         PiBang Linux is derived from Raspbian.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
          <a href="#cite_note-82">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           82
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </li>
        <li>
         Raspbian for Robots  is a
         <a href="/wiki/Fork_(software_development)" title="Fork (software development)">
          fork
         </a>
         of Raspbian for robotics projects with LEGO, Grove, and Arduino.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
          <a href="#cite_note-83">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           83
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </li>
     </ul>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Driver_APIs">
       Driver APIs
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Driver APIs">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raspberrypi_video_API_03.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="659" data-file-width="707" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Raspberrypi_video_API_03.svg/220px-Raspberrypi_video_API_03.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Raspberrypi_video_API_03.svg/330px-Raspberrypi_video_API_03.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Raspberrypi_video_API_03.svg/440px-Raspberrypi_video_API_03.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raspberrypi_video_API_03.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Scheme of the implemented
        <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" title="Application programming interface">
         APIs
        </a>
        :
        <a href="/wiki/OpenMAX" title="OpenMAX">
         OpenMAX
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/OpenGL_ES" title="OpenGL ES">
         OpenGL ES
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/OpenVG" title="OpenVG">
         OpenVG
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Raspberry Pi can use a
      <a href="/wiki/VideoCore" title="VideoCore">
       VideoCore
      </a>
      IV
      <a href="/wiki/Graphics_processing_unit" title="Graphics processing unit">
       GPU
      </a>
      via a
      <a href="/wiki/Binary_blob" title="Binary blob">
       binary blob
      </a>
      , which is loaded into the GPU at boot time from the
      <a href="/wiki/Secure_Digital" title="Secure Digital">
       SD-card
      </a>
      , and additional software, that initially was
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_source_software" title="Closed source software">
       closed source
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h-online_2011_110-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-h-online_2011-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This part of the driver code was later released,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARMuserland_111-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ARMuserland-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      however much of the actual driver work is done using the closed source GPU code. Application software use calls to closed source run-time libraries (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OpenMax" title="OpenMax">
       OpenMax
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/OpenGL_ES" title="OpenGL ES">
       OpenGL ES
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/OpenVG" title="OpenVG">
       OpenVG
      </a>
      ) which in turn calls an open source driver inside the Linux kernel, which then calls the closed source VideoCore IV GPU driver code. The
      <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" title="Application programming interface">
       API
      </a>
      of the kernel driver is specific for these closed libraries. Video applications use
      <a href="/wiki/OpenMAX" title="OpenMAX">
       OpenMAX
      </a>
      ,
      <span class="nowrap">
       <a href="/wiki/3D_computer_graphics" title="3D computer graphics">
        3D applications
       </a>
      </span>
      use
      <a href="/wiki/OpenGL_ES" title="OpenGL ES">
       OpenGL ES
      </a>
      and
      <span class="nowrap">
       <a href="/wiki/2D_computer_graphics" title="2D computer graphics">
        2D applications
       </a>
      </span>
      use
      <a href="/wiki/OpenVG" title="OpenVG">
       OpenVG
      </a>
      which both in turn use
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EGL_(OpenGL)" title="EGL (OpenGL)">
       EGL
      </a>
      . OpenMAX and EGL use the open source kernel driver in turn.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Third_party_application_software">
       Third party application software
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Third party application software">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Mathematica" title="Mathematica">
        Mathematica
       </a>
        Since 21 November 2013, Raspbian includes a full installation of this proprietary software for free.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
        <a href="#cite_note-113">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         113
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
        <a href="#cite_note-114">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         114
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 1 August 2014
       <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , the version is Mathematica 10.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         115
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Minecraft" title="Minecraft">
        Minecraft
       </a>
        Released 11 February 2013, a version for the Raspberry Pi, in which you can modify the game world with code.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         116
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/UserGate_Web_Filter" title="UserGate Web Filter">
        UserGate Web Filter
       </a>
        On September 20, 2013, Florida-based security vendor Entensys announced porting UserGate Web Filter to Raspberry Pi platform.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         117
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Julia_(programming_language)" title="Julia (programming language)">
        Julia
       </a>
        Since May 2015, Julia programming language compiles and runs on the Pi 2, and the original, in an alpha state.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tracking_Raspberry_Pi_online_on_a_global_map">
       Tracking Raspberry Pi online on a global map
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Tracking Raspberry Pi online on a global map">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Ryan Walmsley, a UK school student created a site in 2012, to register and track any Raspberry Pi across the globe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rastrack_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rastrack-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It became very popular soon after its launch.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The current site is powered by
      <a href="/wiki/Google_Maps" title="Google Maps">
       Google Maps
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/DigitalOcean" title="DigitalOcean">
       Digital Ocean
      </a>
      and is free. It has a limitation of registering only one Raspberry Pi per unique email id. It uses
      <a href="/wiki/Internet_Protocol" title="Internet Protocol">
       IP
      </a>
      based basic location tracking and is fairly accurate up to Locale or City level.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reception_and_use">
       Reception and use
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Reception and use">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Technology writer
      <a href="/wiki/Glyn_Moody" title="Glyn Moody">
       Glyn Moody
      </a>
      described the project in May 2011 as a "potential
      <span class="nowrap">
       <a href="/wiki/BBC_Micro" title="BBC Micro">
        BBC Micro
       </a>
       2.0
      </span>
      ", not by replacing
      <span class="nowrap">
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PC_compatible" title="PC compatible">
        PC compatible
       </a>
      </span>
      machines but by supplementing them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computerworlduk_as_british_120-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-computerworlduk_as_british-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In March 2012 Stephen Pritchard echoed the BBC Micro successor sentiment in
      <i>
       ITPRO
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-itpro_bbc_2012_121-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-itpro_bbc_2012-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Alex Hope, co-author of the Next Gen report, is hopeful that the computer will engage children with the excitement of programming.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph_122-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-telegraph-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Co-author
      <a href="/wiki/Ian_Livingstone" title="Ian Livingstone">
       Ian Livingstone
      </a>
      suggested that the
      <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">
       BBC
      </a>
      could be involved in building support for the device, possibly branding it as the BBC Nano.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_classroom_coders_77-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-bbc_classroom_coders-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chris Williams, writing in
      <a href="/wiki/The_Register" title="The Register">
       The Register
      </a>
      sees the inclusion of programming languages such as Kids Ruby,
      <a href="/wiki/Scratch_(programming_language)" title="Scratch (programming language)">
       Scratch
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/BASIC" title="BASIC">
       BASIC
      </a>
      as a "good start" to equip kids with the skills needed in the future  although it remains to be seen how effective their use will be.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raspberrypi_model_names_123-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-raspberrypi_model_names-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/The_Centre_for_Computing_History" title="The Centre for Computing History">
       The Centre for Computing History
      </a>
      strongly supports the Raspberry Pi project, feeling that it could "usher in a new era".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computinghistory_donated_124-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-computinghistory_donated-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before release, the board was showcased by
      <a href="/wiki/ARM_Holdings" title="ARM Holdings">
       ARM
      </a>
      's CEO
      <a href="/wiki/Warren_East" title="Warren East">
       Warren East
      </a>
      at an event in Cambridge outlining
      <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">
       Google
      </a>
      's ideas to improve UK science and technology education.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cabume_improve_stem_teaching_125-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cabume_improve_stem_teaching-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Harry Fairhead, however, suggests that more emphasis should be put on improving the educational software available on existing hardware, using tools such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_App_Inventor" title="Google App Inventor">
       Google App Inventor
      </a>
      to return programming to schools, rather than adding new hardware choices.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raspberry_Pi_or_Programming_126-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Raspberry_Pi_or_Programming-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Simon Rockman, writing in a
      <a href="/wiki/ZDNet" title="ZDNet">
       ZDNet
      </a>
      blog, was of the opinion that teens will have "better things to do", despite what happened in the 1980s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zdnet_mid-life_crisis_127-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-zdnet_mid-life_crisis-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In October 2012, the Raspberry Pi won T3's Innovation of the Year award,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and futurist
      <a href="/wiki/Mark_Pesce" title="Mark Pesce">
       Mark Pesce
      </a>
      cited a (borrowed) Raspberry Pi as the inspiration for his
      <a href="/wiki/Ambient_device" title="Ambient device">
       ambient device
      </a>
      project MooresCloud.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
       <a href="#cite_note-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In October 2012, the
      <a href="/wiki/British_Computer_Society" title="British Computer Society">
       British Computer Society
      </a>
      reacted to the announcement of enhanced specifications by stating, "it's definitely something we'll want to sink our teeth into."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcs_2012_130-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bcs_2012-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In February 2015, a
      <a href="/wiki/Switched-mode_power_supply" title="Switched-mode power supply">
       switched-mode power supply
      </a>
      chip, designated U16, of the Raspberry Pi 2 model B version 1.1 (the initially released version) was found to be vulnerable to flashes of light,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U16_forums_131-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-U16_forums-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      particularly the light from
      <a href="/wiki/Flashtube#Xenon" title="Flashtube">
       xenon camera flashes
      </a>
      and green
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U16_Hackaday_132-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-U16_Hackaday-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and red
      <a href="/wiki/Laser_pointer" title="Laser pointer">
       laser pointers
      </a>
      . However, other bright lights, particularly ones that are on continuously, were found to have no effect. The symptom was the Raspberry Pi 2 spontaneously rebooting or turning off when these lights were flashed at the chip. Initially, some users and commenters suspected that the
      <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_pulse" title="Electromagnetic pulse">
       electromagnetic pulse
      </a>
      from the xenon flash tube was causing the problem by interfering with the computer's digital circuitry, but this was ruled out by tests where the light was either blocked by a card or aimed at the other side of the Raspberry Pi 2, both of which did not cause a problem. The problem was narrowed down to the U16 chip by covering first the
      <a href="/wiki/System_on_a_chip" title="System on a chip">
       system on a chip
      </a>
      (main processor) and then U16 with
      <a href="/wiki/Blu-Tack" title="Blu-Tack">
       opaque poster mounting compound
      </a>
      . Light being the sole culprit, instead of EMP, was further confirmed by the laser pointer tests,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U16_Hackaday_132-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-U16_Hackaday-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      where it was also found that less opaque covering was needed to shield against the laser pointers than to shield against the xenon flashes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U16_forums_131-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-U16_forums-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The U16 chip seems to be bare silicon without a plastic cover (i.e. a
      <a href="/wiki/Chip-scale_package" title="Chip-scale package">
       chip-scale package
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wafer-level_package" title="Wafer-level package">
       wafer-level package
      </a>
      ), which would, if present, block the light. Based on the facts that the chip, like all semiconductors, is light-sensitive (
      <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_effect" title="Photovoltaic effect">
       photovoltaic effect
      </a>
      ), that
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
       silicon
      </a>
      is transparent to infrared light, and that xenon flashes emit more infrared light than laser pointers (therefore requiring more light shielding),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U16_forums_131-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-U16_forums-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      it is currently thought that this combination of factors allows the sudden bright infrared light to cause an instability in the output voltage of the power supply, triggering shutdown or restart of the Raspberry Pi 2. Unofficial workarounds include covering U16 with opaque material (such as electrical tape,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U16_forums_131-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-U16_forums-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U16_Hackaday_132-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-U16_Hackaday-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      lacquer, poster mounting compound, or even balled-up bread
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U16_forums_131-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-U16_forums-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ), putting the Raspberry Pi 2 in a case,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U16_Hackaday_132-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-U16_Hackaday-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and avoiding taking photos of the top side of the board with a xenon flash. This issue was not caught before the release of the Raspberry Pi 2 because while commercial electronic devices are routinely subjected to tests of susceptibility to radio interference, it is not standard or common practice to test their susceptibility to optical interference.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U16_forums_131-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-U16_forums-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Community">
       Community
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Community">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Raspberry Pi community was described by Jamie Ayre of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FLOSS" title="FLOSS">
       FLOSS
      </a>
      software company
      <a href="/wiki/AdaCore" title="AdaCore">
       AdaCore
      </a>
      as one of the most exciting parts of the project.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computerweekly_community_2012_133-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-computerweekly_community_2012-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Community blogger Russell Davis said that the community strength allows the Foundation to concentrate on documentation and teaching.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computerweekly_community_2012_133-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-computerweekly_community_2012-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The community developed a
      <a href="/wiki/Fanzine" title="Fanzine">
       fanzine
      </a>
      around the platform called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_MagPi" title="The MagPi">
        The MagPi
       </a>
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thedigitallifestyle_dixon_2012_134-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-thedigitallifestyle_dixon_2012-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which in 2015, was handed over to the Raspberry Pi Foundation by its volunteers to be continued in-house.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-all_change_meet_the_new_magpi_135-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-all_change_meet_the_new_magpi-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A series of community
      <i>
       Raspberry Jam
      </i>
      events have been held across the UK and around the world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raspberry_jam_136-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-raspberry_jam-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_education">
       Use in education
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Use in education">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      As of January 2012
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , enquiries about the board in the United Kingdom have been received from schools in both the
      <a href="/wiki/State_school" title="State school">
       state
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Private school (United Kingdom)">
       private
      </a>
      sectors, with around five times as much interest from the latter. It is hoped that businesses will sponsor purchases for less advantaged schools.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_reboot_computing_137-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-guardian_reboot_computing-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The CEO of Premier Farnell said that the government of a country in the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">
       Middle East
      </a>
      has expressed interest in providing a board to every schoolgirl, in order to enhance her employment prospects.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_700-per-sec_2012_138-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-guardian_700-per-sec_2012-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2014, the Raspberry Pi Foundation hired a number of its community members including ex-teachers and software developers to launch a set of free learning resources for its website.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-welcome_to_our_website_140-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-welcome_to_our_website-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The resources are freely licensed under Creative Commons, and contributions and collaborations are encouraged on social coding platform
      <a href="/wiki/GitHub" title="GitHub">
       GitHub
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The Foundation also started a teacher training course called Picademy with the aim of helping teachers prepare for teaching the new computing curriculum using the Raspberry Pi in the classroom.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-picademy_free_cpd_for_teachers_141-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-picademy_free_cpd_for_teachers-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The continued professional development course is provided free for teachers and is run by the Foundation's education team.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reviews">
       Reviews
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Reviews">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Raspberry Pi model B rev. 1 was rated 4/5 by
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.pcmag.com/article2/0,2817,2407058,00.asp" rel="nofollow">
       PCMag
      </a>
      , while Raspberry Pi model B rev. 2 was rated 4.1/5 by
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.board-db.org/detail.php?boardid=3" rel="nofollow">
       Board-DB.org
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-style ambox-Prose" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
