     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raspberry_Pi_board_at_TransferSummit_2011_cropped.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1120" data-file-width="1440" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Raspberry_Pi_board_at_TransferSummit_2011_cropped.jpg/220px-Raspberry_Pi_board_at_TransferSummit_2011_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Raspberry_Pi_board_at_TransferSummit_2011_cropped.jpg/330px-Raspberry_Pi_board_at_TransferSummit_2011_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Raspberry_Pi_board_at_TransferSummit_2011_cropped.jpg/440px-Raspberry_Pi_board_at_TransferSummit_2011_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raspberry_Pi_board_at_TransferSummit_2011_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An early alpha-test board in operation using different layout from later beta and production boards
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 2006, early concepts of the Raspberry Pi were based on the
      <a href="/wiki/Atmel" title="Atmel">
       Atmel
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ATmega" title="ATmega">
       ATmega
      </a>
      644 microcontroller. Its schematics and
      <a href="/wiki/Printed_circuit_board" title="Printed circuit board">
       PCB
      </a>
      layout are publicly available.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ubergizmo_build_your_own_142-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ubergizmo_build_your_own-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Foundation
      <a href="/wiki/Trustee" title="Trustee">
       trustee
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Eben_Upton" title="Eben Upton">
       Eben Upton
      </a>
      assembled a group of teachers, academics and computer enthusiasts to devise a computer to inspire children.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_reboot_computing_137-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-guardian_reboot_computing-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The computer is inspired by Acorn's
      <a href="/wiki/BBC_Micro" title="BBC Micro">
       BBC Micro
      </a>
      of 1981.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rpi_bbcsucc_143-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rpi_bbcsucc-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessweekly_blown_2012_144-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-businessweekly_blown_2012-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pi's model A, model B and model B+ are references to the original models of the British educational
      <a href="/wiki/BBC_Micro" title="BBC Micro">
       BBC Micro
      </a>
      computer, developed by
      <a href="/wiki/Acorn_Computers" title="Acorn Computers">
       Acorn Computers
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raspberrypi_model_names_123-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-raspberrypi_model_names-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first ARM prototype version of the computer was mounted in a package the same size as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USB_memory_stick" title="USB memory stick">
       USB memory stick
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It had a USB port on one end and an
      <a href="/wiki/HDMI" title="HDMI">
       HDMI
      </a>
      port on the other.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Foundation's goal was to offer two versions, priced at US$25 and US$35. They started accepting orders for the higher priced model B on 29 February 2012,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawler_146-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lawler-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the lower cost model A on 4 February 2013.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the even lower cost (US$20) A+ on 10 November 2014.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A-Plus-Announcement_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-A-Plus-Announcement-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-launch">
       Pre-launch
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Pre-launch">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <span class="vevent">
        July2011
        <span style="display:none">
         (
         <span class="bday dtstart published updated">
          2011-07
         </span>
         )
        </span>
        
        <span class="summary">
         Trustee
         <a href="/wiki/Eben_Upton" title="Eben Upton">
          Eben Upton
         </a>
         publicly approached the
         <a href="/wiki/RISC_OS_Open" title="RISC OS Open">
          RISC OS Open
         </a>
         community in July 2011 to enquire about assistance with a port.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riscosopen_eben_2011_148-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-riscosopen_eben_2011-148">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           148
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         Adrian Lees at Broadcom has since worked on the port,
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riscoscode_2011_149-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-riscoscode_2011-149">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           149
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riscosopen_2011_150-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-riscosopen_2011-150">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           150
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         with his work being cited in a discussion regarding the graphics drivers.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raspberrypi_blob_broadcom_engineer_151-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-raspberrypi_blob_broadcom_engineer-151">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           151
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         This port is now included in NOOBS.
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>
      <li>
       August 2011  50 alpha boards are manufactured. These boards were functionally identical to the planned model B,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
        <a href="#cite_note-152">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         152
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       but they were physically larger to accommodate debug headers. Demonstrations of the board showed it running the
       <a href="/wiki/LXDE" title="LXDE">
        LXDE
       </a>
       desktop on Debian,
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quake_3" title="Quake 3">
         Quake 3
        </a>
       </i>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/1080p" title="1080p">
        1080p
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         153
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/1080p" title="1080p">
        Full HD
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/MPEG-4" title="MPEG-4">
        MPEG-4
       </a>
       video over HDMI.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         154
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       October 2011  A version of
       <span class="nowraplinks">
        <a href="/wiki/RISC_OS" title="RISC OS">
         RISC OS 5
        </a>
       </span>
       was demonstrated in public, and following a year of development the port was released for general consumption in November 2012.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osnews_risc_os_74-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-osnews_risc_os-74">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         74
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archive_london_show_2011_155-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-archive_london_show_2011-155">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         155
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iconbar_newsround_156-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-iconbar_newsround-156">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         156
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RaspbettyP_RISC_OS_157-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-RaspbettyP_RISC_OS-157">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         157
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       December 2011  Twenty-five model B Beta boards were assembled and tested
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
        <a href="#cite_note-158">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         158
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       from one hundred unpopulated PCBs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
        <a href="#cite_note-159">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         159
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The component layout of the Beta boards was the same as on production boards. A single error was discovered in the board design where some pins on the CPU were not held high; it was fixed for the first production run.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
        <a href="#cite_note-160">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         160
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Beta boards were demonstrated booting Linux, playing a 1080p movie trailer and the
       <a href="/wiki/Rightware" title="Rightware">
        Rightware
       </a>
       Samurai
       <a href="/wiki/OpenGL_ES" title="OpenGL ES">
        OpenGL ES
       </a>
       benchmark.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
        <a href="#cite_note-161">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         161
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Early 2012  During the first week of the year, the first 10 boards were put up for auction on
       <a href="/wiki/EBay" title="EBay">
        eBay
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
        <a href="#cite_note-162">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         162
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theregister_yours_for_just_163-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-theregister_yours_for_just-163">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         163
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       One was bought anonymously and donated to the museum at
       <a href="/wiki/The_Centre_for_Computing_History" title="The Centre for Computing History">
        The Centre for Computing History
       </a>
       in Suffolk, England.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computinghistory_donated_124-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-computinghistory_donated-124">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         124
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-techweekeurope_production_164-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-techweekeurope_production-164">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         164
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The ten boards (with a total retail price of 220) together raised over 16,000,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
        <a href="#cite_note-165">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         165
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       with the last to be auctioned, serial number No. 01, raising 3,500.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
        <a href="#cite_note-166">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         166
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       In advance of the anticipated launch at the end of February 2012, the Foundation's servers struggled to cope with the load placed by watchers repeatedly refreshing their browsers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cabume_early_wednesday_launch_167-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cabume_early_wednesday_launch-167">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         167
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">
       Launch
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Launch">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raspberry_Pi_-_Model_A.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Raspberry_Pi_-_Model_A.jpg/220px-Raspberry_Pi_-_Model_A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Raspberry_Pi_-_Model_A.jpg/330px-Raspberry_Pi_-_Model_A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Raspberry_Pi_-_Model_A.jpg/440px-Raspberry_Pi_-_Model_A.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raspberry_Pi_-_Model_A.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Raspberry Pi model A
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <ul>
      <li>
       19 February 2012  The first proof of concept SD card image that could be loaded onto an SD card to produce a preliminary operating system is released. The image was based on
       <a href="/wiki/Debian" title="Debian">
        Debian
       </a>
       6.0 (Squeeze), with the
       <a href="/wiki/LXDE" title="LXDE">
        LXDE
       </a>
       desktop and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midori_browser" title="Midori browser">
        Midori browser
       </a>
       , plus various programming tools. The image also runs on
       <a href="/wiki/QEMU" title="QEMU">
        QEMU
       </a>
       allowing the Raspberry Pi to be emulated on various other platforms.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
        <a href="#cite_note-168">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         168
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-POC-image_169-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-POC-image-169">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         169
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       29 February 2012  Initial sales commence 29 February 2012
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_on_sale_170-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbc_on_sale-170">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         170
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       at 06:00UTC;. At the same time, it was announced that the model A, originally to have had 128MB of RAM, was to be upgraded to 256MB before release.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawler_146-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-lawler-146">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         146
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Foundation's website also announced: "Six years after the project's inception, we're nearly at the end of our first run of development  although it's just the beginning of the Raspberry Pi story."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thehindu_2012_171-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-thehindu_2012-171">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         171
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Online_shopping" title="Online shopping">
        web-shops
       </a>
       of the two licensed manufacturers selling Raspberry Pi's within the United Kingdom,
       <a href="/wiki/Premier_Farnell" title="Premier Farnell">
        Premier Farnell
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/RS_Components" title="RS Components">
        RS Components
       </a>
       , had their websites stalled by heavy web traffic immediately after the launch (RS Components briefly going down completely).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arstechnica_retailers_toppled_172-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-arstechnica_retailers_toppled-172">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         172
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_with_program_2012_173-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-guardian_with_program_2012-173">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         173
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unconfirmed reports suggested that there were over two million expressions of interest or pre-orders.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
        <a href="#cite_note-174">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         174
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The official Raspberry Pi Twitter account reported that Premier Farnell sold out within a few minutes of the initial launch, while RS Components took over 100,000 pre orders on day one.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawler_146-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-lawler-146">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         146
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Manufacturers were reported in March 2012 to be taking a "healthy number" of pre-orders.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computerweekly_community_2012_133-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-computerweekly_community_2012-133">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         133
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       March 2012  Shipping delays for the first batch were announced in March 2012, as the result of installation of an incorrect Ethernet port,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raspberrypi_hiccup_2012_175-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-raspberrypi_hiccup_2012-175">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         175
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ibtimes_hiccup_2012_176-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ibtimes_hiccup_2012-176">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         176
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       but the Foundation expected that manufacturing quantities of future batches could be increased with little difficulty if required.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ibtimes_upton_interview_2012_177-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ibtimes_upton_interview_2012-177">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         177
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       "We have ensured we can get them [the Ethernet connectors with magnetics] in large numbers and Premier Farnell and RS Components [the two distributors] have been fantastic at helping to source components," Upton said. The first batch of 10,000 boards was manufactured in Taiwan and China.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tax-news.com_balks_178-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tax-news.com_balks-178">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         178
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-civilsociety_manufacture_abroad_179-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-civilsociety_manufacture_abroad-179">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         179
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       8 March 2012  Release Raspberry Pi Fedora Remix, the recommended Linux distribution,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
        <a href="#cite_note-180">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         180
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       developed at
       <a href="/wiki/Seneca_College" title="Seneca College">
        Seneca College
       </a>
       in Canada.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbc_181-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cbc-181">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         181
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       March 2012  The Debian port is initiated by Mike Thompson, former CTO of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomz" title="Atomz">
        Atomz
       </a>
       . The effort was largely carried out by Thompson and Peter Green, a volunteer Debian developer, with some support from the Foundation, who tested the resulting binaries that the two produced during the early stages (neither Thompson nor Green had physical access to the hardware, as boards were not widely accessible at the time due to demand).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-volunteers_182-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ars-volunteers-182">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         182
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the preliminary
       <a href="/wiki/Proof_of_concept" title="Proof of concept">
        proof of concept
       </a>
       image distributed by the Foundation before launch was also Debian-based, it differed from Thompson and Green's Raspbian effort in a couple of ways. The POC image was based on then-
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debian_stable" title="Debian stable">
        stable
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debian_Squeeze" title="Debian Squeeze">
        Debian Squeeze
       </a>
       , while Raspbian aimed to track then-upcoming
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debian_Wheezy" title="Debian Wheezy">
        Debian Wheezy
       </a>
       packages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-POC-image_169-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-POC-image-169">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         169
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Aside from the updated packages that would come with the new release, Wheezy was also set to introduce the armhf architecture,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armhf_183-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-armhf-183">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         183
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       which became the
       <i>
        raison d'tre
       </i>
       for the Raspbian effort. The Squeeze-based POC image was limited to the armel architecture, which was, at the time of Squeeze's release, the latest attempt by the Debian project to have Debian run on the newest ARM
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EABI" title="EABI">
        EABI
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armel_184-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-armel-184">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         184
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armhf" title="Armhf">
        armhf
       </a>
       architecture in Wheezy intended to make Debian run on the ARM
       <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture#Floating-point_.28VFP.29" title="ARM architecture">
        VFP
       </a>
       hardware
       <a href="/wiki/Floating-point_unit" title="Floating-point unit">
        floating-point unit
       </a>
       , while armel was limited to emulating floating point operations in software.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-connors-arm_185-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-connors-arm-185">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         185
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vfp_186-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-vfp-186">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         186
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since the Raspberry Pi included a VFP, being able to make use of the hardware unit would result in performance gains and reduced power usage for floating point operations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-volunteers_182-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ars-volunteers-182">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         182
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The armhf effort in mainline Debian, however, was orthogonal to the work surrounding the Pi and only intended to allow Debian to run on ARMv7 at a minimum, which would mean the Pi, an ARMv6 device, would not benefit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-armhf_183-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-armhf-183">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         183
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result, Thompson and Green set out to build the 19,000 Debian packages for the device using a custom
       <a href="/wiki/Software_build" title="Software build">
        build
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluster_(computing)" title="Cluster (computing)">
        cluster
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars-volunteers_182-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ars-volunteers-182">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         182
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Post-launch">
       Post-launch
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Post-launch">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       16 April 2012  Reports appear from the first buyers who had received their Raspberry Pi.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
        <a href="#cite_note-187">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         187
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
        <a href="#cite_note-188">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         188
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       20 April 2012  The schematics for the model A and model B are released.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
        <a href="#cite_note-189">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         189
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       18 May 2012  The Foundation reported on its blog about a prototype
       <a href="/wiki/Camera_module" title="Camera module">
        camera module
       </a>
       they had tested.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
        <a href="#cite_note-190">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         190
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The prototype used a
       <span class="nowrap">
        14-
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megapixel" title="Megapixel">
         megapixel
        </a>
       </span>
       module.
      </li>
      <li>
       22 May 2012  Over 20,000 units had been shipped.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rastrack_118-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rastrack-118">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         118
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       16 July 2012  It was announced that 4,000 units were being manufactured per day, allowing Raspberry Pis to be bought in bulk.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-electronista_191-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-electronista-191">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         191
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
        <a href="#cite_note-192">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         192
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       24 August 2012  Hardware accelerated video (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H.264" title="H.264">
        H.264
       </a>
       ) encoding becomes available after it became known that the existing license also covered encoding. Previously it was thought that encoding would be added with the release of the announced camera module.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
        <a href="#cite_note-193">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         193
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
        <a href="#cite_note-194">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         194
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, no stable software exists for hardware H.264 encoding.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195">
        <a href="#cite_note-195">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         195
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       At the same time the Foundation released two additional codecs that can be bought separately,
       <a href="/wiki/MPEG-2" title="MPEG-2">
        MPEG-2
       </a>
       and Microsoft's
       <a href="/wiki/VC-1" title="VC-1">
        VC-1
       </a>
       . Also it was announced that the Pi will implement
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consumer_Electronics_Control#CEC" title="Consumer Electronics Control">
        CEC
       </a>
       , enabling it to be controlled with the television's remote control.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rpi-codec_37-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rpi-codec-37">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         37
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       July 2012  Release of Raspbian.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physorg_owano_2012_196-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-physorg_owano_2012-196">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         196
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       5 September 2012  The Foundation announced a second revision of the Raspberry Pi Model B.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
        <a href="#cite_note-197">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         197
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       A revision 2.0 board is announced, with a number of minor corrections and improvements.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198">
        <a href="#cite_note-198">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         198
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       6 September 2012  Announcement that in future the bulk of Raspberry Pi units would be manufactured in the UK, at
       <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">
        Sony
       </a>
       's manufacturing facility in
       <a href="/wiki/Pencoed" title="Pencoed">
        Pencoed
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">
        Wales
       </a>
       . The Foundation estimated that the plant would produce 30,000 units per month, and would create about 30 new jobs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computerworlduk_dunn_2012_199-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-computerworlduk_dunn_2012-199">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         199
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
        <a href="#cite_note-200">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         200
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       15 October 2012  It is announced that new Raspberry Pi Model Bs are to be fitted with 512MB instead of 256MB RAM.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-512MB_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-512MB-8">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         8
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       24 October 2012  The Foundation announces that "all of the VideoCore driver code which runs on the ARM" had been released as
       <a href="/wiki/Free_software" title="Free software">
        free software
       </a>
       under a
       <a href="/wiki/BSD_licenses" title="BSD licenses">
        BSD-style license
       </a>
       , making it "the first ARM-based multimedia
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/System-on-chip" title="System-on-chip">
        SoC
       </a>
       with fully-functional, vendor-provided (as opposed to partial,
       <a href="/wiki/Reverse_engineering" title="Reverse engineering">
        reverse engineered
       </a>
       ) fully
       <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">
        open-source
       </a>
       drivers", although this claim has not been universally accepted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARMuserland_111-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ARMuserland-111">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         111
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 28 February 2014, they also announced the release of full documentation for the VideoCore IV graphics core, and a complete source release of the graphics stack under a 3-clause BSD license
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2thbirthday_201-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-2thbirthday-201">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         201
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-openvideocore_202-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-openvideocore-202">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         202
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       October 2012  It was reported that some customers of one of the two main distributors had been waiting more than six months for their orders. This was reported to be due to difficulties in sourcing the CPU and conservative sales forecasting by this distributor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zdnet_shead_2012_203-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-zdnet_shead_2012-203">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         203
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       17 December 2012  The Foundation, in collaboration with IndieCity and Velocix, opens the Pi Store, as a "one-stop shop for all your Raspberry Pi (software) needs". Using an application included in
       <a href="#Software">
        Raspbian
       </a>
       , users can browse through several categories and download what they want. Software can also be uploaded for moderation and release.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
        <a href="#cite_note-204">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         204
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       3 June 2013  'New Out Of Box Software or NOOBS is introduced. This makes the Raspberry Pi easier to use by simplifying the installation of an operating system. Instead of using specific software to prepare an SD card, a file is unzipped and the contents copied over to a FAT formatted (4GB or bigger) SD card. That card can then be booted on the Raspberry Pi and a choice of six operating systems is presented for installation on the card. The system also contains a recovery partition that allows for the quick restoration of the installed OS, tools to modify the config.txt and an online help button and web browser which directs to the Raspberry Pi Forums.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noobs_205-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-noobs-205">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         205
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       October 2013  The Foundation announces that the one millionth Pi had been manufactured in the United Kingdom.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206">
        <a href="#cite_note-206">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         206
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       November 2013: they announce that the two millionth Pi shipped between 24 and 31 October.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207">
        <a href="#cite_note-207">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         207
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       28 February 2014  On the day of the second anniversary of the Raspberry Pi, Broadcom, together with the Raspberry PI foundation, announced the release of full documentation for the VideoCore IV graphics core
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2014)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       , and a complete source release of the graphics stack under a 3-clause BSD license.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2thbirthday_201-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-2thbirthday-201">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         201
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-openvideocore_202-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-openvideocore-202">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         202
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raspberry_Pi_Compute_Module.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="238" data-file-width="499" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Raspberry_Pi_Compute_Module.png/220px-Raspberry_Pi_Compute_Module.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Raspberry_Pi_Compute_Module.png/330px-Raspberry_Pi_Compute_Module.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Raspberry_Pi_Compute_Module.png/440px-Raspberry_Pi_Compute_Module.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raspberry_Pi_Compute_Module.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Raspberry Pi Compute Module
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <ul>
      <li>
       7 April 2014  The official Raspberry Pi blog announced the Raspberry Pi Compute Module, a device in the form factor of a 200-pin DDR2
       <a href="/wiki/SO-DIMM" title="SO-DIMM">
        SO-DIMM
       </a>
       memory module (though not in any way compatible with such RAM), intended for consumer electronics designers to use as the core of their own products.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM-Announcement_34-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-CM-Announcement-34">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         34
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       June 2014  The official Raspberry Pi blog mentioned that the three millionth Pi shipped in early May 2014.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208">
        <a href="#cite_note-208">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         208
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       14 July 2014  The official Raspberry Pi blog announced the Raspberry Pi Model B+, "the final evolution of the original Raspberry Pi. For the same price as the original Raspberry Pi model B, but incorporating numerous small improvements people have been asking for".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B-Plus-Announcement_32-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-B-Plus-Announcement-32">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         32
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       10 November 2014  The official Raspberry Pi blog announced the Raspberry Pi Model A+.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A-Plus-Announcement_31-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-A-Plus-Announcement-31">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         31
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is the smallest and cheapest (US$20) Raspberry Pi so far and has the same processor and RAM as the model A. Like the A, it has no Ethernet port, and just one USB port, but does have the other innovations of the B+, like lower power, micro-SD-card slot, and 40-pin HAT compatible GPIO.
      </li>
      <li>
       2 February 2015  The official Raspberry Pi blog announced the Raspberry Pi 2. Looking like a Model B+, it has a 900MHz quad-core ARMv7 Cortex-A7 CPU, twice the memory (for a total of 1GB) and complete compatibility with the original generation of Raspberry Pis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209">
        <a href="#cite_note-209">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         209
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       14 May 2015  As a result of production optimizations the price of Model B+ was decreased from US$35 to US$25.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210">
        <a href="#cite_note-210">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         210
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Skeptics have speculated that the actual reason is competition from lower priced C.H.I.P.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211">
        <a href="#cite_note-211">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         211
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>

         <a href="#cite_ref-MB_2-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Transistorized memory, such as RAM,
         <a href="/wiki/Read-only_memory" title="Read-only memory">
          ROM
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">
          flash
         </a>
         and
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cache_memory" title="Cache memory">
          cache
         </a>
         sizes as well as file sizes are specified using
         <a href="/wiki/Binary_prefix" title="Binary prefix">
          binary meanings
         </a>
         for K (1024
         <sup>
          1
         </sup>
         ), M (1024
         <sup>
          2
         </sup>
         ), G (1024
         <sup>
          3
         </sup>
         ), ...
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-3">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

          <a href="#cite_ref-3">
           ^
          </a>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">
       Further reading
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raspberry_Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Further reading">

       </a>

        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>

       </i>
       ; Sean McManus and Mike Cook; 432 pages; 2013;
       <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781118554210">
        ISBN 978-1118554210
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Getting Started with Raspberry Pi
       </i>
       ; Matt Richardson and Shawn Wallace; 176 pages; 2013;
       <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781449344214">
        ISBN 978-1449344214
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Raspberry Pi User Guide
       </i>
       ; Eben Upton and Gareth Halfacree; 312 pages; 2014;

        ISBN 978-1118921661
       </a>
